reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a standard 2D convolution operation with square input and asymmetric kernel, with dilation and padding.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple): Size of the convolution kernel (height, width). 
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input (top/bottom, left/right). Defaults to (0, 0).
        dilation (tuple, optional): Spacing between kernel elements (height, width). Defaults to (1, 1).
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: int = 1, padding: tuple = (0, 0), dilation: tuple = (1, 1), bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = (5, 9)
width = 512
height = 512
stride = 1
padding = (2, 4)
dilation = (2, 3)

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

Let me draft a kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W, int kh, int kw, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w ) { // Each thread computes one output element: (n, k, oh, ow) int ow = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oh = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int k = blockIdx.z % K; int n = blockIdx.z / K; if (ow >= out_W || oh >= out_H) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { for (int r = 0; r < kh; ++r) { int ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h; for (int s = 0; s < kw; ++s) { int iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { float inp = input[((n * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float w = weight[((k * C + c) * kh + r) * kw + s]; sum += inp * w; } } } } output[((n * K + k) * out_H + oh) * out_W + ow] = sum; } ``` This is a naïve kernel. Let me detail: - Warp handles output channels k0..k7 - Thread i handles N-value n_i - For each k in 0..7: - accumulator[k] stores C[k0+k, n_i] To compute partial sums for a K_TILE: 1. If we load K_TILE=32, then for each N value we need 32 floats. - But each thread only needs the 32 floats for its specific N value. - So each thread loads 32 floats from B sequentially and stores in registers or shared memory. 2. Load A[k0..k7, k'] for k' in K_TILE - This is 8 * 32 = 256 floats - If stored in shared memory, all 32 threads in the warp can access it. 3. Each thread does 8 * 32 = 256 FMAs. For K = 1440, we'd do 1440/32 = 45 iterations. Total FMAs per thread = 8 * 1440 = 11520 FMAs. At 2 FMAs per cycle per SM for FP32 on H100... H100 actually has very high FMA throughput. 11520 FMAs should be quite fast. Now for memory: - B loads: each thread loads 1440 floats for its N value = 5760 bytes - A loads: each warp loads 8 * 1440 = 11520 floats = 46080 bytes - But these are shared across the warp through shared memory - Each thread in warp loads 11520/32 = 360 floats = 1440 bytes - Total memory per thread: ~7200 bytes - For 256 threads per block: ~1.8MB per block But wait, each block handles 256 N values. Total B memory per block = 256 * 1440 * 4 = ~1.4MB. That's too much for shared memory, so B should be loaded incrementally. In the K-tile approach: - For each K_TILE=32: - Each thread loads 32 floats from B for its N value: 128 bytes - Each warp loads 8 * 32 = 256 floats from A: 1024 bytes - These go into shared memory - Compute 256 FMAs per thread - Repeat 45 times So per thread memory traffic: 45 * (128 + 32 bytes for A) ≈ 7200 bytes. At ~2 TB/s memory bandwidth, this is very fast (sub-millisecond). Let me write this kernel. Actually, the weights A can be kept in L2 cache since they're reused across all blocks. Similarly, B elements are unique per N but might benefit from coalescing. For coalescing B loads: - B has shape [K, N] or [N, K] depending on layout. - In N-major order (i.e., B[n, k] or B[k, n]), we want consecutive threads to access consecutive memory. In our design, thread i in a warp handles N-value n_i. For B, we want thread i to access B[k', n_i] where different i have different n_i but the same k'. This means if B is stored as [K, N], then for fixed k', B[k', n_i] are scattered across memory. Bad for coalescing. If B is stored as [N, K], then B[n_i, k'] for fixed k' and varying n_i... still, consecutive threads have different n_i, so if we access B[n_i, k'] with varying n_i, these are far apart. Actually wait - in the implicit GEMM formulation: - B[k, n] where k = c*R*S + r*S + s and n = batch*out_H*out_W + oh*out_W + ow - If we iterate over k in the inner loop, then for a fixed n, B[k, n] is consecutive in k. - But in our kernel, we want to access B[k', n_i] for fixed k' across threads. These are NOT consecutive. Hmm, let me reconsider the access pattern. In the GEMM loop: For each k_tile: For k in k_tile: Load A[k0..k7, k] into smem For each thread i: Load B[k, n_i] into register Compute 8 FMAs Here, for a fixed k, thread 0 loads B[k, n_0], thread 1 loads B[k, n_1], etc. If B is column-major (K, N), these accesses are strided by N, which is very bad. To get coalescing, we need consecutive threads to access consecutive elements. This means for fixed k, consecutive threads should have consecutive n_i values. In our setup, if warp handles N-values n_0, n_1, ..., n_31 where these are consecutive, then: - B[k, n_0], B[k, n_1], ..., B[k, n_31] are consecutive in memory if B is stored as [K, N] with N being the fastest-changing dimension... wait, no. Actually, in C-style row-major: - B[k, n] is at address base + k * N_stride + n - For fixed k and varying n by 1, addresses differ by 1 * sizeof(float) - YES! This is perfectly coalesced! So B should be stored as [K, N] with N being the innermost dimension, i.e., B[k][n]. Wait, in the standard GEMM C = A * B with A[M, K] and B[K, N], row-major: - C[i, j] = sum_k A[i, k] * B[k, j] - For fixed k, B[k, j] varies with j consecutively. So in our kernel, if consecutive threads have consecutive n values, then loading B[k, n] for fixed k and consecutive n gives perfect coalescing. Great! For A: A[m, k] for fixed k and varying m. If m is not consecutive across threads in the same instruction, it might not matter much since A is much smaller and goes into shared memory. Let me now think about the im2col transformation. In implicit GEMM, we don't explicitly create the B matrix; we generate elements on the fly from the input tensor. This saves memory bandwidth. So the kernel computes: For each k in K (which decomposes into c, r, s): B_val = input[n, c, ih, iw] where ih, iw depend on oh, ow, r, s A_val = weight[k, c, r, s] (well, A is weight reshaped) accumulator += A_val * B_val Wait, in standard implicit GEMM, A is weight of shape [K, C*R*S] and B is the im2col matrix of shape [C*R*S, N*P*Q]. So for the k-th row of A (output channel), and for n-th column of B (spatial location): C[k, n] = sum over all (c, r, s) of weight[k, c, r, s] * input[n, c, r, s spatial mapping] In the kernel, rather than forming B explicitly, we can compute B elements on the fly from the original input. For this to work, our thread's n value maps to (batch, oh, ow). Then for each (c, r, s), we compute: ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w input_val = input[batch, c, ih, iw] (or 0 if out of bounds) This means we don't need to load B from global memory in the traditional sense; we compute it from input. For A (weights), we load weight[k, c, r, s] from global memory. Given this, let me rewrite the kernel. Actually, the input is stored as [N, C, H, W] or [N, H, W, C]. In PyTorch default: [N, C, H, W]. For fixed c, r, s: input[n, c, ih, iw]. Different n values map to different (batch, oh, ow), which gives different ih, iw. The addresses input[n, c, ih, iw] are scattered in memory. Bad for coalescing! This is the fundamental problem with direct convolution: input accesses for the same (c, r, s) but different spatial locations are not coalesced. To fix this, we need to either: 1. Use shared memory for the input tile 2. Make threads in a warp handle the same spatial location but different output channels Let me try option 2: - Warp handles 32 output channels and 1 spatial location - 32 threads: each thread handles 1 output channel - 32 accumulators per warp (1 per thread) - All threads in warp access the SAME input spatial locations but different weights For a fixed spatial location and K tile = 32 (which is c,r,s values): - Thread t loads weight[t, 0..31] where t is the output channel - All threads load the SAME 32 input values (broadcast) - Each thread does 32 FMAs - 1440 / 32 = 45 iterations - 45 * 32 = 1440 FMAs per thread This is MUCH less compute per thread (1440 vs 23040), which means more memory bottleneck. But the memory access pattern is better: - All 32 threads in warp load the same input values - these can be satisfied with L1 cache or broadcast - Weight accesses: 32 threads load 32 different weight rows of size 32 each = 1024 floats - With K tile = 32, these can go into shared memory Total memory per thread per iteration: 32 floats from weights (via shared memory) + 32 floats from input (via broadcast/L1) Total FMAs per thread per iteration: 32 Arithmetic intensity: 32 FMAs / (32 + 32) floats = 0.5 FLOPs/byte. VERY memory bound! Since ridge point is ~20 FLOPs/byte, this is extremely memory bound. So it's a tradeoff: - Option A: High compute per thread but uncoalesced input loads - Option B: Coalesced/broadcast loads but low compute per thread For the H100 with 3.35 TB/s bandwidth and 67 TFLOPS FP32, the ridge point is ~20. But empirically we measured ~2577 GB/s and ~51.85 TFLOPS, ridge point ~20. At 0.5 FLOPs/byte, we're very memory bound. We need to increase arithmetic intensity. To do this, we need each thread to do more FMAs per unit of memory loaded. This means: 1. Larger K tiles in shared memory so each input value is reused more 2. Each thread handles more output channels But K = 1440 total. The maximum K tile is 1440 (all). If we load all weights into shared memory: - Weight size: 64 * 1440 * 4 = 360KB. Now, for the shared memory loading: Input shared memory layout: [in_tile_h][in_tile_w][in_channels_in_tile] Where in_channels_in_tile = 8. Actually, let me define: - tile_oh = blockIdx.y * TILE_H, tile_ow = blockIdx.x * TILE_W - For output spatial index loc in 0..255: - oh = tile_oh + loc / TILE_W, ow = tile_ow + loc % TILE_W - For kernel element (r, s): - ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h - iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w When computing from shared memory: - ih_rel = ih - tile_ih_base - iw_rel = iw - tile_iw_base Where tile_ih_base = tile_oh * stride_h - pad_h, tile_iw_base = tile_ow * stride_w - pad_w Hmm, this gets complicated with padding because ih_rel might be negative (pad) or >= in_tile_h. Actually, a cleaner way: - The shared memory stores input for the region [tile_ih_start : tile_ih_end] where: - tile_ih_start = tile_oh * stride_h - pad_h - tile_ih_end = tile_ih_start + in_tile_h - But we need to handle boundaries where tile_ih_start < 0 or tile_ih_end > H. When loading into shared memory: - For global ih in [max(0, tile_ih_start) : min(H, tile_ih_end)]: - smem[ih - tile_ih_start][...] = input[...] - For out-of-bounds regions, smem is set to 0 (or just not accessed if we check bounds). During computation: - ih_rel = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h - tile_ih_start = (tile_oh + loc/TILE_W) * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h - (tile_oh * stride_h - pad_h) = loc/TILE_W * stride_h + r * dilation_h - Wait! With stride=1: ih_rel = loc/TILE_W + r * dilation_h - iw_rel = loc%TILE_W + s * dilation_w That's much simpler! The relative indices in shared memory depend only on the output location within the tile and the kernel offset. For stride=1: - ih_rel = loc_h + r * dilation_h - iw_rel = loc_w + s * dilation_w Where loc_h = loc / TILE_W, loc_w = loc % TILE_W. These are in range: - ih_rel: [0, TILE_H-1] + [0, (kh-1)*dil_h] = [0, TILE_H-1 + (kh-1)*dil_h] - Which equals [0, in_tile_h - 1]. Perfect! So the shared memory layout: - smem[ih_rel][iw_rel][c] for ih_rel in [0, in_tile_h-1], iw_rel in [0, in_tile_w-1] When loading from global memory: - Global ih = tile_ih_start + ih_rel - Global iw = tile_iw_start + iw_rel - If 0 <= global_ih < H and 0 <= global_iw < W, load input - Else set to 0 Now for the compute code within each thread: Each thread handles: - oc_local in [0, 7] (local output channel index in block) - 8 spatial locations: loc = base_loc + j for j in [0, 7] For efficiency, I can unroll the 5x9 kernel and 8 spatial locations. But wait, each thread doing 8 spatial locations * 8 output channels (well, 1 output channel actually since oc_local is fixed per thread) * 5 * 9 * 32 input channels = 8 * 1 * 45 * 32 = 11520 MACs. Actually wait, I said each thread handles 1 oc and 8 spatial locations? Let me recount. Total threads = 256. Block handles: 8 oc * 256 locations = 2048 outputs. 256 threads * 8 outputs each = 2048 outputs. So each thread handles 8 outputs. If distributed as 1 oc * 8 locations, then yes, each thread handles 1 output channel and 8 spatial locations. So: - Thread accumulators: 8 floats (one per spatial location) - For each input channel group (4 groups of 8): - Load input for 8 channels into smem - Load weights for 8 output channels into smem - For each of 8 spatial locations: - For each in_channel in 0..7: - For r in 0..4, s in 0..8: - loc_h = loc / 16, loc_w = loc % 16 - ih = loc_h + r * 2, iw = loc_w + s * 3 - inp = smem[ih][iw][in_channel] - w = w_smem[oc][in_channel][r][s] - accum[loc_idx] += inp * w The issue is that different threads in a warp might access different smem locations for ih, iw, causing some bank conflicts. But with 8 channels in the inner dimension, bank conflicts depend on how threads access them. Wait, if consecutive threads have different spatial locations but access smem[ih][iw][in_channel], then for fixed in_channel, they're accessing different (ih, iw) addresses. The bank depends on the address. Since ih and iw differ, bank conflicts are likely but not necessarily severe. To reduce bank conflicts, we can use a different shared memory layout, e.g., [c][ih][iw] instead of [ih][iw][c]. Then consecutive threads accessing the same c but different (ih, iw) would have addresses c*ih_max*iw_max + ih*iw_max + iw. Consecutive iw values give consecutive banks (with some stride), which might be better after the 4-byte float offset. Hmm, shared memory bank width on H100 is 4 bytes. So float accesses to consecutive addresses map to consecutive banks. If thread i accesses address base + i * stride, bank conflicts occur when stride causes multiple threads to hit the same bank. In our case, if consecutive threads have the same in_channel but different (ih, iw), then with layout [c][ih][iw]: address = (c * ih_max * iw_max + ih * iw_max + iw) * sizeof(float) For consecutive threads with same in_channel and varying (ih, iw), the address differences are (delta_ih * iw_max + delta_iw) * 4. With 32 banks and bank width 4 bytes: - If two threads access addresses that differ by a multiple of 128 bytes (32 banks * 4 bytes), they conflict. - delta_ih * iw_max * 4 + delta_iw * 4 = multiple of 128 - delta_ih * iw_max + delta_iw = multiple of 32 With iw_max = 40 (for 16x16 tile): delta_ih * 40 + delta_iw ≡ 0 (mod 32) This can happen for various combinations, so some bank conflicts are possible but not severe. Actually, for NVIDIA GPUs, if threads in a warp access addresses that fall in the same 128-byte sector, they can be coalesced. For shared memory, each bank can handle one request per cycle, but a single thread can access one bank per cycle. If multiple threads access the same bank for different 32-bit words, it takes multiple cycles. To be safe, we can interleave/shared memory padding. Or we can just see if performance is acceptable. Since the kernel is 5x9, let's also unroll these loops. We have 5 and 9 as constants, so we can use pragma unroll or manual unrolling. Actually, let me write a cleaner kernel. I'll use implicit GEMM but with a slight modification. Wait, I just realized: I should write the kernel and try it. Let me draft it and compile. Actually, I realize I should first write a simpler but well-optimized kernel to see the baseline, then iterate. Let me draft: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define TILE_H 16 #define TILE_W 16 #define BLOCK_OC 8 #define BLOCK_IC 8 __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W) { // Block indices int block_n = blockIdx.z; // batch index int block_oh = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int block_ow = blockIdx.x * TILE_W; int block_oc = blockIdx.w * BLOCK_OC; // Thread indices int tid = threadIdx.x; int oc_local = tid % BLOCK_OC; int sg = tid / BLOCK_OC; // spatial group (0..31) int base_loc = sg * 8; // each thread handles 8 spatial locations // Calculate input tile bounds int in_tile_h = TILE_H + (5 - 1) * 2; // = 24 int in_tile_w = TILE_W + (9 - 1) * 3; // = 40 int tile_ih_start = block_oh - 2; // -pad_h int tile_iw_start = block_ow - 4; // -pad_w // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: [BLOCK_IC][in_tile_h][in_tile_w] for input // Then: [BLOCK_OC][BLOCK_IC][5][9] for weights float* smem_input = smem; // size: BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w float* smem_weight = smem + BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; // size: BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * 5 * 9 int smem_in_size = BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; int smem_w_size = BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * 5 * 9; // Accumulators float accum[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; // Main loop over input channels in groups of BLOCK_IC for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < C; ic_base += BLOCK_IC) { // Load input into shared memory // Total elements: BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w = 8 * 24 * 40 = 7680 // 256 threads, so each thread loads 30 elements #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < smem_in_size; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int rem = i % (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int ih = rem / in_tile_w; int iw = rem % in_tile_w; int global_ih = tile_ih_start + ih; int global_iw = tile_iw_start + iw; int global_c = ic_base + c; float val = 0.0f; if (global_n >= 0 && global_n < N && global_c >= 0 && global_c < C && global_ih >= 0 && global_ih < H && global_iw >= 0 && global_iw < W) { val = input[((global_n * C + global_c) * H + global_ih) * W + global_iw]; } smem_input[i] = val; } // Load weights into shared memory // Total: BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * 45 = 2880 // 256 threads, each loads ~11 elements #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < smem_w_size; i += blockDim.x) { // Decode: i -> [oc][ic][r][s] int oc = i / (BLOCK_IC * 45); int rem1 = i % (BLOCK_IC * 45); int ic = rem1 / 45; int rem2 = rem1 % 45; int r = rem2 / 9; int s = rem2 % 9; int global_oc = block_oc + oc; int global_ic = ic_base + ic; float val = 0.0f; if (global_oc < K && global_ic < C) { val = weight[((global_oc * C + global_ic) * 5 + r) * 9 + s]; } smem_weight[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute // Each thread: oc_local, base_loc..base_loc+7 int global_oc = block_oc + oc_local; if (global_oc < K) { #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int loc = base_loc + j; int loc_h = loc / TILE_W; int loc_w = loc % TILE_W; int oh = block_oh + loc_h; int ow = block_ow + loc_w; if (oh < out_H && ow < out_W) { #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < BLOCK_IC; ic++) { #pragma unroll for (int r = 0; r < 5; r++) { #pragma unroll for (int s = 0; s < 9; s++) { int ih = loc_h + r * 2; int iw = loc_w + s * 3; float inp = smem_input[(ic * in_tile_h + ih) * in_tile_w + iw]; float w = smem_weight[((oc_local * BLOCK_IC + ic) * 5 + r) * 9 + s]; accum[j] += inp * w; } } } } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output int global_oc = block_oc + oc_local; if (global_oc < K) { #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int loc = base_loc + j; int loc_h = loc / TILE_W; int loc_w = loc % TILE_W; int oh = block_oh + loc_h; int ow = block_ow + loc_w; if (oh < out_H && ow < out_W) { int out_idx = ((block_n * K + global_oc) * out_H + oh) * out_W + ow; output[out_idx] = accum[j]; } } } } ``` Wait, I see some issues: 1. `global_n` is not defined in the input loading. Let me write the code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define TILE_H 16 #define TILE_W 16 #define BLOCK_OC 8 #define BLOCK_IC 8 __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W, int kh, int kw, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w) { int in_tile_h = TILE_H + (kh - 1) * dilation_h; int in_tile_w = TILE_W + (kw - 1) * dilation_w; int out_W_tiles = (out_W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int out_H_tiles = (out_H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H; int spatial_tiles = out_H_tiles * out_W_tiles; int n = blockIdx.x / spatial_tiles; int rem = blockIdx.x % spatial_tiles; int oh_tile = rem / out_W_tiles; int ow_tile = rem % out_W_tiles; int tile_oh = oh_tile * TILE_H; int tile_ow = ow_tile * TILE_W; int tile_oc = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_OC; int tid = threadIdx.x; int oc_local = tid % BLOCK_OC; int sg = tid / BLOCK_OC; int base_loc = sg * 8; int tile_ih_start = tile_oh * stride_h - pad_h; int tile_iw_start = tile_ow * stride_w - pad_w; extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_in_size = BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; float* smem_input = smem; float* smem_weight = smem + smem_in_size; int smem_w_size = BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * kh * kw; float accum[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < C; ic_base += BLOCK_IC) { // Load input tile for (int i = tid; i < smem_in_size; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int rem2 = i % (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int ih = rem2 / in_tile_w; int iw = rem2 % in_tile_w; int global_ih = tile_ih_start + ih; int global_iw = tile_iw_start + iw; int global_c = ic_base + c; float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && global_c < C && global_ih >= 0 && global_ih < H && global_iw >= 0 && global_iw < W) { val = input[((n * C + global_c) * H + global_ih) * W + global_iw]; } smem_input[i] = val; } // Load weights for (int i = tid; i < smem_w_size; i += blockDim.x) { int oc = i / (BLOCK_IC * kh * kw); int rem1 = i % (BLOCK_IC * kh * kw); int ic = rem1 / (kh * kw); int rem2 = rem1 % (kh * kw); int r = rem2 / kw; int s = rem2 % kw; int global_oc = tile_oc + oc; int global_ic = ic_base + ic; float val = 0.0f; if (global_oc < K && global_ic < C) { val = weight[((global_oc * C + global_ic) * kh + r) * kw + s]; } smem_weight[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); int global_oc = tile_oc + oc_local; if (global_oc < K) { #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int loc = base_loc + j; int loc_h = loc / TILE_W; int loc_w = loc % TILE_W; int oh = tile_oh + loc_h; int ow = tile_ow + loc_w; if (oh < out_H && ow < out_W) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < BLOCK_IC; ic++) { for (int r = 0; r < kh; r++) { for (int s = 0; s < kw; s++) { int ih = loc_h * stride_h + r * dilation_h; int iw = loc_w * stride_w + s * dilation_w; float inp = smem_input[((ic * in_tile_h + ih) * in_tile_w) + iw]; float w = smem_weight[((((oc_local * BLOCK_IC + ic) * kh) + r) * kw) + s]; sum += inp * w; } } } accum[j] += sum; } } } __syncthreads(); } int global_oc = tile_oc + oc_local; if (global_oc < K) { float b = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[global_oc] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int loc = base_loc + j; int loc_h = loc / TILE_W; int loc_w = loc % TILE_W; int oh = tile_oh + loc_h; int ow = tile_ow + loc_w; if (oh < out_H && ow < out_W) { int out_idx = ((n * K + global_oc) * out_H + oh) * out_W + ow; output[out_idx] = accum[j] + b; } } } } torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w, int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w, int64_t dilation_h, int64_t dilation_w) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(0); int kh = weight.size(2); int kw = weight.size(3); int out_H = (H + 2 * pad_h - dilation_h * (kh - 1) - 1) / stride_h + 1; int out_W = (W + 2 * pad_w - dilation_w * (kw - 1) - 1) / stride_w + 1; auto output = torch::empty({N, K, out_H, out_W}, input.options()); int out_H_tiles = (out_H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H; int out_W_tiles = (out_W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int spatial_tiles = out_H_tiles * out_W_tiles; int spatial_blocks = N * spatial_tiles; int k_tiles = (K + BLOCK_OC - 1) / BLOCK_OC; dim3 grid(spatial_blocks, k_tiles); dim3 block(256); int in_tile_h = TILE_H + (kh - 1) * dilation_h; int in_tile_w = TILE_W + (kw - 1) * dilation_w; int smem_size = (BLOCK_IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w + BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * kh * kw) * sizeof(float); const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; conv2d_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, K, out_H, out_W, kh, kw, stride_h, stride_w, pad_h, pad_w, dilation_h, dilation_w); return output; } """ class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=(0,0), dilation=(1,1), bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size if isinstance(kernel_size, tuple) else (kernel_size, kernel_size) self.stride = stride if isinstance(stride, tuple) else (stride, stride) self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding) self.dilation = dilation if isinstance(dilation, tuple) else (dilation, dilation) self.use_bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels, in_channels, self.kernel_size[0], self.kernel_size[1])) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if bias: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x): if not x.is_cuda: x = x.cuda() if self.weight.is_cuda: self.weight = self.weight.cuda() if self.bias is not None: self.bias = self.bias.cuda() weight = self.weight bias = self.bias if self.use_bias else None return conv2d_cuda.conv2d_forward( x, weight, bias, self.stride[0], self.stride[1], self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.dilation[0], self.dilation[1] ) ``` Wait, there's an issue. `load_inline` needs to be called at module load time, so I need to define it before the class. Actually, one important thing: in CUDA, the shared memory layout `smem_input[((ic * in_tile_h + ih) * in_tile_w) + iw]` with `in_tile_w` might have bank conflicts. And the weight loading uses linear indexing `smem_weight[i]` which is fine since all threads load consecutively. For the input shared memory, when computing: ```cuda float inp = smem_input[((ic * in_tile_h + ih) * in_tile_w) + iw]; ``` Threads in a warp have the same `ic` (they're in the same loop iteration over ic), but different `ih` and `iw` because they have different `loc_h` and `loc_w`. With 8 threads per `oc_local` but different `sg`, threads in the same warp might have the same `sg` or different. Wait, thread IDs in a warp are 0..31. - oc_local = tid % 8 = 0..7 - sg = tid / 8 = 0..3 So in a warp: - 4 groups of 8 threads each - Each group has sg = 0, 1, 2, or 3 - Within each group, threads have different oc_local (0..7) but the same sg So for a fixed `ic` and `r`, `s`: - Threads in the warp have different `loc` values (base_loc + j where loc depends on sg) - Actually, within a group of 8 threads with the same sg, they have the same `loc` values but different `oc_local`! - So they access the exact same `smem_input` addresses for `inp`. - This is a broadcast: all 8 threads read the same shared memory address. This is handled efficiently by shared memory (broadcast capability). And threads in different sg groups (same warp) have different `loc` values, so they access different `ih`, `iw` addresses. With 4 different `sg` values, there are 4 different `(ih, iw)` pairs being accessed simultaneously. The address formula: `((ic * in_tile_h + ih) * in_tile_w) + iw` For fixed `ic`, address depends on `ih * in_tile_w + iw`. With `in_tile_w = 40`, `ih = loc_h + r*dil_h`, `iw = loc_w + s*dil_w`. For 4 threads with different sg (0..3): - sg=0: loc = 0..7, various loc_h, loc_w - sg=1: loc = 8..15, various loc_h, loc_w - sg=2: loc = 16..23 - sg=3: loc = 24..31 Actually, wait. All 8 threads in the sg group have the SAME loc values? No, each thread computes 8 locs: base_loc + j for j=0..7, but each j is processed in a separate iteration of the `j` loop, not simultaneously! Ah, I see. The `#pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++)` loop means each thread processes its 8 spatial locations sequentially. So at any given time during compute, a thread is working on ONE spatial location. Within a warp at a specific instruction: - 8 threads (sg=0, different oc): working on loc=0..7? No, wait. For the test with (5, 9), I can specialize the kernel using templates and dispatch at runtime. Let me try a hybrid approach: 1. Write a templated kernel with KH, KW, DILATION_H, DILATION_W, STRIDE_H, STRIDE_W as template parameters 2. At runtime, instantiate the template for the specific values 3. Also provide a generic fallback for other values For the test case: KH=5, KW=9, DIL_H=2, DIL_W=3, STRIDE_H=1, STRIDE_W=1. Template dispatching: ```cpp template <int KH, int KW, int DILATION_H, int DILATION_W, int STRIDE_H, int STRIDE_W> __global__ void conv2d_kernel_t(...) { ... } // In the host function: if (kh == 5 && kw == 9 && dilation_h == 2 && dilation_w == 3 && stride_h == 1 && stride_w == 1) { conv2d_kernel_t<5, 9, 2, 3, 1, 1><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); } else { // generic kernel conv2d_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); } ``` This way, the hot path gets full unrolling. But wait, the dilation affects `in_tile_h` and `in_tile_w` and the address calculations. With template parameters, these become compile-time constants, and the inner loops can be fully unrolled. Actually, `in_tile_h = TILE_H + (KH-1)*DILATION_H` is a compile-time constant with templates. And `ih = loc_h * STRIDE_H + r * DILATION_H` uses compile-time multiplies. This is very simple and less error-prone. kernel: ``` __global__ void conv2d_simple( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W, int kh, int kw, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w) { int total = N * K * out_H * out_W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode idx into n, oc, oh, ow int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % out_W; tmp /= out_W; int oh = tmp % out_H; tmp /= out_H; int oc = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int n = tmp; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int r = 0; r < kh; r++) { for (int s = 0; s < kw; s++) { int ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h; int iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w; float inp = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { inp = input[((n * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } float w = weight[((oc * C + c) * kh + r) * kw + s]; sum += inp * w; } } } if (bias != nullptr) sum += bias[oc]; output[idx] = sum; } ``` This is simple, easy to verify, and guaranteed correct. <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n__device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) {\n uint32_t u = __float_as_uint(x);\n u &= 0xFFFFE000u;\n return __uint_as_float(u);\n}\n\n__global__ void conv2d_kernel_tf32(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W,\n int K, int out_H, int out_W,\n int kh, int kw,\n int stride_h, int stride_w,\n int pad_h, int pad_w,\n int dilation_h, int dilation_w)\n{\n int total = N * K * out_H * out_W;\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n\n int tmp = idx;\n int ow = tmp % out_W; tmp /= out_W;\n int oh = tmp % out_H; tmp /= out_H;\n int oc = tmp % K; tmp /= K;\n int n = tmp;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) {\n for (int r = 0; r < kh; r++) {\n for (int s = 0; s < kw; s++) {\n int ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h;\n int iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w;\n float inp = 0.0f;\n if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) {\n inp = input[((n * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw];\n }\n float w = weight[((oc * C + c) * kh + r) * kw + s];\n sum += round_tf32(inp) * round_tf32(w);\n }\n }\n }\n if (bias != nullptr) sum += bias[oc];\n output[idx] = sum;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor conv2d_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias,\n int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w,\n int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w,\n int64_t dilation_h, int64_t dilation_w) {\n \n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int K = weight.size(0);\n int kh = weight.size(2);\n int kw = weight.size(3);\n \n int out_H = (H + 2 * pad_h - dilation_h * (kh - 1) out_W;\n const int threads = 512;\n int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;\n \n const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr;\n \n If bit 12 is 1, we round up. But if all lower bits are 0 and bit 13 is 0 (even), we don't round up. This is complex. Actually, a simple approach: ```cpp __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { // Use inline PTX or just let the compiler do it // For tensor cores, tf32 rounding is hardware // For software, we can approximate: float y = x * 512.0f; // scale so tf32 lsb becomes 1.0 y = roundf(y); // round to nearest integer y = y / 512.0f; // scale back return y; } ``` But `roundf` is slow. Let me use bit manipulation: Actually, the simplest correct round-to-nearest-even for tf32 is: ```cpp __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { uint32_t u = __float_as_uint(x); uint32_t mantissa = u & 0x1FFF; // lower 13 bits if (mantissa > 0x1000 || (mantissa == 0x1000 && (u & 0x2000))) { u += 0x2000; } u &= 0xFFFFE000u; return __uint_as_float(u); } ``` Hmm, this might not handle overflows correctly. And it's getting complicated. Actually, for practical purposes, truncation vs round-to-nearest for tf32 differs by at most 1 ulp in the tf32 representation. The tf32 ulp at value ~1 is 2^-10 ≈ 0.001. Let me look up a simpler way to do round-to-nearest-even for tf32 in software: Actually, there's a known trick: ```cpp __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { uint32_t u = __float_as_uint(x); // Add 0x1000 to bit 12 for rounding u += 0x1000; // Keep only upper bits u &= 0xFFFFE000u; return __uint_as_float(u); } ``` Wait, does this implement round-to-nearest? Let's see: - If bit 12 and lower are >= 0x1000, adding 0x1000 causes a carry into bit 13. - If bit 12 and lower are < 0x1000, adding 0x1000 doesn't carry into bit 13. - But this is actually round-half-up, not round-to-nearest-even. For tie-breaking (when lower 13 bits = 0x1000), we want to round to even, which means keeping bit 13 as is. If bit 13 is 0, we round down (truncate). If bit 13 is 1, we round up. Round-half-up always rounds up on tie. The difference between round-half-up and round-to-nearest-even only matters for ties, which are rare (1 in 4096 for random data). So for practical purposes, round-half-up is very close to round-to-nearest-even. Let me just use `u = (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000` for simplicity. For random data with ~1440 multiply-adds in fp32, what's the expected difference between two different accumulation orders? If all terms are positive and around 0.5, the sum is ~720. When adding 0.5 to 720, the result 720.5 might not be exactly representable in fp32. The exact spacing at 720: 2^(floor(log2(720)) - 23) = 2^(9 - 23) = 2^-14 = 6.1e-5. 720.5 = 720 + 0.5. In binary: 720 = 1011010000 0.5 = 0.1 720.5 = 1011010000.1 In fp32: mantissa is 23 bits. 720 = 1.011010000 * 2^9 Moving the binary point: 1.01101000010000000000000 (23 bits after the point) Wait, let me compute more carefully: 720 in binary: 1011010000 = 1.011010000 * 2^9 0.5: in the same alignment: 2^-1, so at exponent 9, 0.5 = 2^-10 of the mantissa unit. The mantissa for 720 is: bits: 011010000 + 14 more bits (since we shifted 9 places) Actually, 720 = 512 + 128 + 64 + 16 = 2^9 + 2^7 + 2^6 + 2^4 In normalized form: 1.011010000 * 2^9 For 720.5: 1.0110100001 * 2^9 In fp32: mantissa has 23 bits. The number 1.0110100001 needs the leading 1 (implicit) plus 10 bits for the visible part, plus the rest zeros. So 720.5 is exactly representable! It's 1.0110100001 followed by 13 zeros. What about 720.25? 720.25 = 1.01101000001 * 2^9. Also exactly representable. In fact, all multiples of 2^(9-23) = 2^-14 = 6.1e-5 are exactly representable around 720. So for sums around 720, adding terms of 0.5, 0.25, etc. is exact as long as the term is a multiple of 6.1e-5. Our terms are input * weight, where input ~ U[0,1] and weight ~ some distribution. These products are generally NOT exact multiples of 6.1e-5. So there will be rounding when adding them. But the key point: for two different accumulation orders of the SAME set of numbers, the final result can differ. How much? This depends on the data. For sums with many cancellations, the error can be large. But for all positive numbers, the error is bounded. Actually, there's a theorem: for summing n non-negative fp32 numbers, the result of any insertion-order summation has relative error at most n * epsilon (for some definition of epsilon). For n=1440, epsilon=2^-23≈1.2e-7, the relative error bound is ~1.7e-4. For a sum of 720, absolute error ~0.12. That's much larger than 1e-4! But this is the worst case. For random numbers of similar magnitude, the typical error is much smaller. Still, 0.0005 = 5e-4 is within the worst-case bound, but it seems larger than what I'd expect for typical random data. Let me try to estimate more carefully. For n terms of magnitude ~a, added to a partial sum that's ~O(n*a): At each step, the rounding error is ~partial_sum * epsilon. If terms are all positive, partial_sum grows from 0 to n*a. The worst-case error is roughly epsilon * integral of partial_sum ds from 0 to n, which is epsilon * (n*a) * n / 2? Hmm, that's not right either. Actually, the forward error for recursive summation is bounded by: |sum_exact - sum_fp| <= (n-1) * epsilon * sum(|x_i|) For all positive x_i, sum(|x_i|) = S. So error <= (n-1) * epsilon * S. For n=1440, epsilon=1.2e-7, S=720: Error <= 1439 * 1.2e-7 * 720 ≈ 0.125 This is the worst case. For two different orderings, the difference between their results is at most the sum of their individual errors, so at most ~0.25. But for typical data, the error is usually much smaller: O(epsilon * sqrt(n) * S) or something like that. For random uniform [0,1] terms, the partial sum after k terms has mean k/2 and variance k/12. The typical rounding error at step k is about epsilon * k/2. The total accumulated error has variance sum of (epsilon * k/2)^2 * Var(rounding) over k. This is getting too theoretical. OK, let me just write the test kernel. ```python kernel_code = """ import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { unsigned int u = __float_as_uint(x); u = (u + 0x1000u) & 0xFFFFE000u; return __uint_as_float(u); } __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W, int kh, int kw, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w) { int total = N * K * out_H * out_W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % out_W; tmp /= out_W; int oh = tmp % out_H; tmp /= out_H; int oc = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int n = tmp; double sum = 0.0; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int r = 0; r < kh; r++) { for (int s = 0; s < kw; s++) { int ih = oh * stride_h - pad_h + r * dilation_h; int iw = ow * stride_w - pad_w + s * dilation_w; double inp = 0.0; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { inp = (double)input[((n * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } double w = (double)weight[((oc * C + c) * kh + r) * kw + s]; sum += (double)round_tf32((float)inp) * (double)round_tf32((float)w); } } } if (bias != nullptr) sum += (double)bias[oc]; output[idx] = (float)sum; } ... Actually, I realize I should also make sure `round_tf32` is applied correctly. For `inp` which is 0.0f for out-of-bounds (padding), `round_tf32(0.0f)` should be `0.0f`. Let me verify: `__float_as_uint(0.0f)` = `0x00000000` `(0 + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000` = `0x00001000 & 0xFFFFE000` = `0x00000000` `__uint_as_float(0)` = `0.0f` Wait! `0x00001000 & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x00000000`. So 0 stays 0. Good. For 1.0f: `__float_as_uint(1.0f)` = `0x3F800000` `0x3F800000 + 0x1000` = `0x3F810000` `0x3F810000 & 0xFFFFE000` = `0x3F810000` Wait, 1.0f in binary exponent/mantissa: exponent = 127 (0x7F), mantissa = 0. So bits: 0 01111111 000...000 In hex: 0x3F800000 Adding 0x1000: 0x3F800000 + 0x1000 = 0x3F810000 Anding with 0xFFFFE000: 0x3F810000 & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x3F810000 So 1.0f becomes 0x3F810000 = 1.0009765625 Wait, that's not right. 1.0f rounded to tf32 should stay 1.0f because it already has zero lower mantissa bits. What went wrong? The addition of 0x1000 caused an overflow into higher bits! Let me look at 1.0f = 0x3F800000: - Bits 31: sign = 0 - Bits 30-23: exponent = 0x7F = 127 - Bits 22-0: mantissa = 0 Adding 0x1000: 0x3F800000 +0x00001000 =0x3F810000 Bit 12 of the full 32-bit word corresponds to... let me think. The mantissa starts at bit 0 (for the fractional part). Actually, float format: - Bit 31: sign - Bits 30-23: exponent (8 bits) - Bits 22-0: mantissa (23 bits) So bit 12 is within the mantissa. For exponent 127, the value of bit 12 is 2^(127-127-23+12) = 2^-11 ≈ 0.000488. So adding 0x1000 to 1.0f adds 2^-11 to the value: 1.0 + 2^-11 = 1.00048828125 Then AND with 0xFFFFE000: 0x3F810000 & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x3F810000 So the result is 1.00048828125, not 1.0! That's wrong! The issue is that adding 0x1000 to 1.0f rounds it UP even though the lower 13 bits are all zero. For round-to-nearest-even, if the lower 13 bits are exactly 0 (a tie), and bit 13 is 0 (even), we should round down (keep as is). My rounding formula `u = (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000` always rounds ties up. For 1.0f, this is incorrect. To fix this, I need tie-breaking logic: ```cpp __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { unsigned int u = __float_as_uint(x); unsigned int lower = u & 0x1FFF; // lower 13 bits unsigned int tie = (lower == 0x1000) && ((u & 0x2000) == 0); if (tie) { // Round to even: truncate u = u & 0xFFFFE000; } else { // Round half up u = (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000; } return __uint_as_float(u); } ``` Wait, if tie and bit 13 is 0 (even), we round down (truncate). If tie and bit 13 is 1 (odd), we round up. If not tie and lower > 0x1000, we round up. If not tie and lower < 0x1000, we round down (which happens naturally from the & operation after adding 0x1000? No, for lower < 0x1000, adding 0x1000 doesn't carry into bit 13, so (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000 = u & 0xFFFFE000... wait that's wrong too. Actually, without addition, just truncation: `u & 0xFFFFE000` With addition: `(u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000` For lower < 0x1000: - Adding 0x1000 doesn't carry to bit 13, so upper bits stay the same, and lower bits become lower + 0x1000 (with some possibly exceeding 13 bits and being truncated). But since lower < 0x1000, lower + 0x1000 < 0x2000. After & 0xFFFFE000, if lower + 0x1000 >= 0x2000, bit 13 is set. WAIT. 0x1000 + 0x0FFF = 0x1FFF < 0x2000, so bit 13 stays 0. 0x1000 + 0x1000 = 0x2000, bit 13 becomes 1. Ah! So for lower < 0x1000, `u + 0x1000` doesn't change bit 13, and `& 0xFFFFE000` truncates. So the result is `u` with lower 13 bits truncated, but bit 13 is unchanged. This is actually correct for round-down. For lower > 0x1000, `u + 0x1000` DOES increment bit 13 (and possibly higher bits). Then `& 0xFFFFE000` truncates. This is correct for round-up. For lower == 0x1000, `u + 0x1000` increments bit 13. We want round-to-nearest-even, so if bit 13 was 0, we should NOT increment it. If bit 13 was 1, we should increment it (making it 0 with a carry to bit 14). So tie-breaking: - If lower == 0x1000 AND (u & 0x2000) == 0: just truncate (don't add) - Else: add and truncate But wait, when we just truncate without adding, it's `u & 0xFFFFE000`. When we add and truncate, it's `(u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000`. For the tie case with bit13=1 (odd): - We want to round up, so we do add-and-truncate, which increments bit 13 (making it 0) and carries to bit 14. Wait, bit 13 is part of the mantissa. Incrementing it: - If bit 13 was 1 and we add 0x1000, the lower bits (including bit 13) are cleared and bit 14 is incremented. - If bit 14 was 0, it becomes 1. If bit 14 was 1, it carries further. This is all handled correctly by `(u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000` when lower == 0x1000. For tie with bit13=0: - We should NOT increment bit 13 - `u & 0xFFFFE000` leaves bit 13 as 0 So the logic is: ```cpp __device__ inline float round_tf32(float x) { unsigned int u = __float_as_uint(x); unsigned int lower = u & 0x1FFF; if (lower < 0x1000) { // Truncate down return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } else if (lower > 0x1000) { // Round up return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { // Tie: round to even (bit 13 determines even/odd) if (u & 0x2000) { // Odd, round up return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { // Even, round down return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } } } ``` Wait, for `lower < 0x1000`: is truncation always correct? If lower = 0x0FFF, the true value is closer to truncation (0x0000) than to 0x2000 (the next tf32 value). Yes, truncation is correct. For `lower > 0x1000`: e.g., lower = 0x1001, the true value is closer to 0x2000, so we round up. For `lower = 0x1000`: exactly midway. Round to even. But wait, what about when lower > 0x1000 but `u + 0x1000` overflows into the exponent? We need to handle that. Actually, `__uint_as_float` handles this correctly because it just interprets the bits. If the addition causes the exponent to increment, that's normal float behavior. However, there's a subtle issue: `u + 0x1000` might overflow from maximum normal float to infinity, or from subnormal to normal. These are edge cases that shouldn't matter for our data. OK, this round_tf32 is getting complex. But for our purposes, maybe the simple version is good enough. What if the tf32 issue is not the main problem? What if I can write a kernel that matches the reference perfectly just by using the right accumulation order? For a simple serial kernel, the accumulation order is fully deterministic within each thread. If cuDNN also uses a serial accumulation (e.g., in direct conv mode), then a matching accumulation order would give identical results. For direct conv in cuDNN, the accumulation order depends on how the loops are structured. If cuDNN does: ```cpp for c in 0..C-1: for h in 0..KH-1: for w in 0..KW-1: accumulate ``` And my kernel does the same, the results should match (assuming both use fp32 or both use tf32). If cuDNN does implicit GEMM, the accumulation order is K-major: ```cpp for k_block in 0..K/K_BLOCK-1: for each output element: accumulate products for this k_block ``` This is different from the serial nested-loop order. For 1440 products, serial order does C-major accumulation. GEMM order does K-major with tile accumulation. Actually, for implicit GEMM, the products are accumulated in chunks along K. For each chunk, a partial result is computed and added to the running total. Within each chunk, the order depends on the specific GEMM implementation. If cuDNN uses cuBLAS for the GEMM, and cuBLAS uses tensor cores with tf32, the accumulation is done in a very specific hardware-defined order. This is hard to match exactly in software. So matching cuDNN exactly is extremely difficult if it uses implicit GEMM + tensor cores. Given all this analysis, I think the most practical approach is: 1. unsigned int lower = u & 0x1FFF; if (lower < 0x1000) { return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } else if (lower > 0x1000) { return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { // tie if (u & 0x2000) { return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } } } __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int out_H, int out_W, int kh, int kw, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w) { int total = N * K * out_H * out_W; } } } if (bias != nullptr) sum += bias[oc]; output[idx] = sum; } torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w, int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w, int64_t dilation_h, int64_t dilation_w) { int N = input.size(0); const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; conv2d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, K, out_H, out_W, kh, kw, stride_h, stride_w, pad_h, pad_w, dilation_h, dilation_w); return output; } """ conv2d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv2d_cuda_simple", cpp_sources="torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w, int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w, int64_t dilation_h, int64_t dilation_w);", cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["conv2d_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=(0, 0), dilation=(1, 1), bias=False): super().__init__() temp_conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias) self.weight = nn.Parameter(temp_conv.weight.data) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(temp_conv.bias.data) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = temp_conv.kernel_size self.stride = temp_conv.stride self.padding = temp_conv.padding self.dilation = temp_conv.dilation def forward(self, x): pad_h = self.padding[0] if isinstance(self.padding, tuple) else self.padding pad_w = self.padding[1] if len(self.padding) > 1 else (self.padding[0] if isinstance(self.padding, tuple) else self.padding) return conv2d_cuda.conv2d_forward( x, self.weight, self.bias, self.stride[0] if isinstance(self.stride, tuple) else self.stride, self.stride[1] if isinstance(self.stride, tuple) else self.stride, pad_h, pad_w, self.dilation[0] if isinstance(self.dilation, tuple) else self.dilation, self.dilation[1] if isinstance(self.dilation, tuple) else self.dilation, ) ``` Wait, I need to be careful with padding. For n=1440 terms added in two different orders, the maximum fp32 rounding difference in double precision is... well, double is exact, so the difference between two orders is exactly the mathematical difference of the two orderings. And we're at 0x3F803000, which is exactly in between. Bit 13 of 0x3F803000 is 1 (since 0x3000 has bit 13 = 1). So we round up to 0x3F804000. But round-to-nearest-even should round to the nearest EVEN tf32 value. 0x3F802000 has mantissa ...0010 (bit 13 = 0, even). 0x3F804000 has mantissa ...0100 (bit 13 = 1, wait no, bit 14 = 1). Actually, tf32 has 10 mantissa bits. So the "even" refers to the last mantissa bit of tf32, which is bit 12. Wait, no. For tf32, the representable numbers have the form: sign * 2^exponent * (1.m9 m8 m7 m6 m5 m4 m3 m2 m1 m0) The "ulp" of tf32 is 2^-10 * 2^exponent. Two adjacent tf32 numbers differ by 2^-10 * 2^exponent. Round-to-nearest-even for tf32 means: If the value is exactly halfway between two tf32 numbers, round to the one whose last tf32 mantissa bit (bit corresponding to 2^-10) is 0 (even). For exponent 0 (value around 1), the last tf32 mantissa bit is 2^-10, which corresponds to bit 13 in the full mantissa (since bits 22 down to 13 are the tf32 mantissa, plus bit 12 which is the guard bit). Wait, let me think again. fp32 mantissa: 23 bits (b22 b21 ... b0) tf32 mantissa: 10 bits (b22 b21 ... b13) For rounding to tf32, we look at bits b12 b11 ... b0. If this is > 0x1000 (half ulp, which is bit 12), we round up. If it's < 0x1000, we round down. If it's exactly 0x1000, we check bit 13 (the last tf32 mantissa bit). If bit 13 is 0 (even), round down. If bit 13 is 1 (odd), round up. Wait, I think my implementation might be wrong. Let me reconsider. For a tie (lower 13 bits = 0x1000), the rounding should depend on bit 13 of the full 32-bit word. In my implementation, I check `u & 0x2000`. 0x2000 = bit 13. So if bit 13 is set (u & 0x2000 != 0), I round up. If bit 13 is clear, I round down. But round-to-nearest-even says: round to the even tf32 value. An even tf32 value has its least significant bit (which is bit 13) equal to 0. So: - If bit 13 is 0, the CURRENT tf32 value (truncated) is even. We should round DOWN (keep it). - If bit 13 is 1, the current tf32 value is odd. We should round UP to make it even. My implementation: ```cpp if (u & 0x2000) { // bit 13 is 1, round up return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { // bit 13 is 0, round down return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } ``` Wait, this is actually BACKWARDS! If bit 13 is 1 (current value is ODD), we should round UP to the NEXT tf32 value, which will be EVEN. If bit 13 is 0 (current value is EVEN), we should round DOWN (keep the current value, which is already even). My implementation does: - bit 13 is 1: round up. Correct! - bit 13 is 0: round down. Correct! Wait, but I was confused earlier. Let me reread: ```cpp if (u & 0x2000) { return __uint_as_float((u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000); } else { return __uint_as_float(u & 0xFFFFE000); } ``` If bit 13 is 1 (odd): returns (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000. This increments bit 13 (making it 0) and clears lower bits. If bit 13 is 0 (even): returns u & 0xFFFFE000. This keeps bit 13 as 0 and clears lower bits. Yes! This is correct for round-to-nearest-even! Example: 0x3F803000 (tie, bit 13 = 1, odd) Returns: (0x3F803000 + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x3F804000 & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x3F804000 0x3F804000 has bit 13 = 0 (since 0x4000 has bit 14 = 1, bit 13 = 0). Even! Correct. Example: 0x3F801000 (tie, bit 13 = 0, even) Returns: 0x3F801000 & 0xFFFFE000 = 0x3F800000 0x3F800000 has bit 13 = 0. Even! Correct.
